Hey guys, OR nurse here and I work with the Stealth Navigation system every so often when I work in the Neuro service. I thought you might be interested in an explanation of how the system works, since it's essentially the same technology that's use for the Xbox Kinect and Motion Capture animation! First, you get multiple MRI's of your head so the surgeon has a large composite of reference during surgery. From those MRI's, the Stealth System can build a 3D model of the surface of you head in it's system. When it's time for surgery, they affix you head to a device that will keep you head perfectly still (our facility uses a Mayfield Head Holder, google at your own risk). Once you head is immobile (fixed in space), the Stealth representative aims the detection device at your head (essentially an Xbox Kinect) and the surgeon uses and instrument with 4 specifically placed motion capture balls at the end of it to calibrate the system. Once calibrated, the system can tell EXTREMELY precisely where the tip of the surgeons tool exists in 3D space. Then, using the multiple composites of MRI's they take and the 3D model of your head, the system shows the surgeon EXACTLY where the tip of their instruments are inside your brain in real time. This way, the surgeon can navigate to the tumor virtually, activate their instruments when they need to cut it out, all while avoiding the major areas of the brain.  Tom Scott does a great video about the system here: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8Gk7EvCQGoo

I was an insurance agent from 2008-2018 (it was my second job), largely selling Medicare insurance to those who qualified (people on permanent disability or 65+), but I originally started in selling private insurance policies to people who needed it, and I've pretty much seen firsthand how all the major insurers will attempt to screw people over at almost any opportunity. I was appointed with basically every major insurer in the country and worked at a brokerage that received calls from all over the US. What insurance companies spend a *significant* portion on isn't care or coordinating care, but in administration (IE, paying themselves, their agents. There was a time 10 years ago where I sold a Medicare Advantage plan with a $0 premium to the member, but I received over $500 for enrolling the person, and I was the bottom-most person in the downstream for this, so just imagine what people were making over me).  Who remembers what insurance was like before the Affordable Care Act passed? I remember the father of a friend of mine having the same insurance policy for two decades, and he wouldn't switch for anything because he kept getting grandfathered in whenever things changed. When he was involved in a major accident that resulted in a 2-3 week hospital stay, he found out *after the fact* that his insurance policy canceled on him after the emergency surgery he had. Why? Because the policy had a $1 million lifetime maximum clause in it. He tried to go out and get another insurance policy but nothing would cover any of his hospital stay because it was a "pre-existing" condition. So for the rest of his life, anything involving a condition as a result from that accident wouldn't have been covered by basically any insurer I ever worked with, until the ACA went into effect in 2013 and made pre-existing condition exclusions a non-issue. He wound up declaring bankruptcy.  We need major fucking healthcare reform in this country. Everyone else can figure this shit out except us.  Anyway, Thick, I'm pulling for you. You're my favorite member of the Neebs crew, and you drive the fun and the stories of the playthroughs more than anyone will give you credit for. Keep fighting, Maverick.

You're probably drowning in advice and have a plan already. But just in case you're down to take in more of the same: 14 years ago, my 46 year old dad was diagnosed with a brain tumor (an oligodendroglioma). He also underwent surgery; biopsy came back cancerous, 100% chance of regrowth, 5 year life expectancy. He cut out sugar, processed foods, and drastically reduced his carbohydrate intake. He's also a godly man, which (at the very least) helped him weather the emotional storm. He's alive and kicking today. He's never done chemo or radiation, had one additional surgery to remove a small regrowth last year. His doctors tell him his life is a miracle.   I hope this helps.
